mission\n<div class="" style="width:100%; text-align:center; border-top:1px #aaa solid"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Discovery_program" title="Discovery program">Discovery program</a></div><div style="width:100%"><div class="noprint" style="float: left; text-align:left;padding-right:0.5em;">&#8592;&#160;<i><a href="/wiki/MESSENGER" title="MESSENGER">MESSENGER</a></i></div><div class="noprint" style="float: right; text-align:right;padding-left:0.5em;"><i><a href="/wiki/Dawn_(spacecraft)" title="Dawn (spacecraft)">Dawn</a></i>&#160;&#8594;</div></div>&#160;</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><i><b>Deep Impact</b></i> was a <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> <a href="/wiki/Space_probe" title="Space probe">space probe</a> launched from <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral_Air_Force_Station" title="Cape Canaveral Air Force Station">Cape Canaveral Air Force Station</a> on January 12, 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SFN_Over_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-SFN_Over-4">[4]</a></sup> It was designed to study the interior composition of the <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comet</a> <a href="/wiki/Tempel_1" title="Tempel 1">Tempel 1</a> (9P/Tempel), by releasing an impactor into the comet. At 05:52 UTC on July 4, 2005, the Impactor successfully collided with the comet\'s <a href="/wiki/Comet_nucleus" title="Comet nucleus">nucleus</a>. The impact excavated debris from the interior of the nucleus, forming an impact crater. Photographs taken by the spacecraft showed the comet to be more dusty and less icy than had been expected. The impact generated an unexpectedly large and bright dust cloud, obscuring the view of the impact crater.\n</p><p>Previous space missions to comets, such as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giotto_mission" title="Giotto mission">Giotto</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Deep_Space_1" title="Deep Space 1">Deep Space 1</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Stardust_(spacecraft)" title="Stardust (spacecraft)">Stardust</a></i>, were fly-by missions. These missions were able to photograph and examine only the surfaces of cometary nuclei, and even then from considerable distances. The <i>Deep Impact</i> mission was the first to eject material from a comet\'s surface, and the mission garnered considerable publicity from the media, international scientists, and amateur astronomers alike.\n</p><p>Upon the completion of its primary mission, proposals were made to further utilize the spacecraft. Consequently, <i>Deep Impact</i> flew by Earth on December 31, 2007 on its way to an extended mission, designated <a href="/wiki/EPOXI" title="EPOXI">EPOXI</a>, with a dual purpose to study <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extrasolar_planet" title="Extrasolar planet">extrasolar planets</a> and comet <a href="/wiki/103P/Hartley" title="103P/Hartley">Hartley 2</a> (103P/Hartley).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASASSE_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASASSE-5">[5]</a></sup> Communication was unexpectedly lost in September 2013 while the craft was heading for another asteroid flyby.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" mission</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_goals">Scientific goals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Scientific goals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The <i>Deep Impact</i> mission was planned to help answer fundamental questions about comets, which included what makes up the composition of the comet\'s nucleus, what depth the crater would reach from the impact, and where the comet originated in its formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Milems_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Milems-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASAQ&amp;A_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASAQ&amp;A-7">[7]</a></sup> By observing the composition of the comet, astronomers hoped to determine how comets form based on the differences between the interior and exterior makeup of the comet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSDC_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSSDC-8">[8]</a></sup> Observations of the impact and its aftermath would allow astronomers to attempt to determine the answers to these questions.\n</p><p>The mission\'s Principal Investigator was <a href="/wiki/Michael_A%27Hearn" title="Michael A\'Hearn">Michael A\'Hearn</a>, an astronomer at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Maryland,_College_Park" title="University of Maryland, College Park">University of Maryland</a>. He led the science team, which included members from <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell University</a>, University of Maryland, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Arizona" title="University of Arizona">University of Arizona</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brown_University" title="Brown University">Brown University</a>, Belton Space Exploration Initiatives, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/JPL" title="JPL">JPL</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Hawaii" title="University of Hawaii">University of Hawaii</a>, <a href="/wiki/Science_Applications_International_Corporation" title="Science Applications International Corporation">SAIC</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ball_Aerospace" title="Ball Aerospace">Ball Aerospace</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Max-Planck-Institut_f%C3%BCr_extraterrestrische_Physik" title="Max-Planck-Institut f&#252;r extraterrestrische Physik">Max-Planck-Institut f&#252;r extraterrestrische Physik</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Spacecraft_design_and_instrumentation">Spacecraft design and instrumentation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Spacecraft design and instrumentation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:90855main_dispcrft.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="765" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/90855main_dispcrft.svg/170px-90855main_dispcrft.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/90855main_dispcrft.svg/255px-90855main_dispcrft.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/90855main_dispcrft.svg/340px-90855main_dispcrft.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:90855main_dispcrft.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Spacecraft overview</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft" title="Spacecraft">spacecraft</a> consists of two main sections, the 372-kilogram (820&#160;lb) copper-core "Smart Impactor" that impacted the comet, and the 601&#160;kg (1,325&#160;lb) "Flyby" section, which imaged the comet from a safe distance during the encounter with Tempel 1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-presskit_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-presskit-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASADeepImpactOverview_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASADeepImpactOverview-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASAimpactor_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASAimpactor-10">[10]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Flyby spacecraft is about 3.3 meters (10.8&#160;ft) long, 1.7 meters (5.6&#160;ft) wide and 2.3 meters (7.5&#160;ft) high.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-presskit_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-presskit-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Milems_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Milems-6">[6]</a></sup> It includes two solar panels, a debris shield, and several science instruments for <a href="/wiki/Speckle_imaging" title="Speckle imaging">imaging</a>, <a href="/wiki/Infrared_spectroscopy" title="Infrared spectroscopy">infrared spectroscopy</a>, and optical navigation to its destination near the comet. The spacecraft also carried two cameras, the <a href="/wiki/High_Resolution_Coronal_Imager" title="High Resolution Coronal Imager">High Resolution Imager</a> (HRI), and the Medium Resolution Imager (MRI). The HRI is an imaging device that combines a visible-light camera with a filter wheel, and an imaging <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> <a href="/wiki/Spectrometer" title="Spectrometer">spectrometer</a> called the "Spectral Imaging Module" or SIM that operates on a spectral band from 1.05 to 4.8 micrometres. It has been optimized for observing the comet\'s nucleus. The MRI is the backup device, and was used primarily for navigation during the final 10-day approach. It also has a filter wheel, with a slightly different set of filters.\n</p><p>The Impactor section of the spacecraft contains an instrument that is optically identical to the MRI, called the Impactor Targeting Sensor (ITS), but without the filter wheel. Its dual purpose was to sense the Impactor\'s trajectory, which could then be adjusted up to four times between release and impact, and to image the comet from close range. As the Impactor neared the comet\'s surface, this camera took high-resolution pictures of the nucleus (as good as 0.2 meters per <a href="/wiki/Pixel" title="Pixel">pixel</a> [7.9&#160;in/px]) that were transmitted in real-time to the Flyby spacecraft before it and the Impactor were destroyed. The final image taken by the Impactor was snapped only 3.7 seconds before impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA2_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA2-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Impactor\'s payload, dubbed the "Cratering Mass", was 100% copper, with a weight of 100&#160;kg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA20_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA20-12">[12]</a></sup> Including this cratering mass, copper formed 49% of total mass of the Impactor (with aluminium at 24% of the total mass);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASAImpactor_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASAImpactor-13">[13]</a></sup> this was to minimize interference with scientific measurements. Since copper was not expected to be found on a comet, scientists could ignore copper\'s signature in any spectrometer readings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA20_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA20-12">[12]</a></sup> Instead of using explosives, it was also cheaper to use copper as the payload.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASAQ&amp;A_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASAQ&amp;A-7">[7]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Explosives would also have been superfluous. At its closing velocity of 10.2&#160;km/s, the Impactor\'s kinetic energy was equivalent to 4.8 metric tons of TNT, considerably more than its actual mass of only 372&#160;kg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASAtelcom_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASAtelcom-14">[14]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The mission coincidentally shared its name with the 1998 film, <i><a href="/wiki/Deep_Impact_(film)" title="Deep Impact (film)">Deep Impact</a></i>, in which a comet strikes the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ABCNews2_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-ABCNews2-15">[15]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_profile">Mission profile</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Mission profile">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Deep_Impact_HRI_tests.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2025" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Deep_Impact_HRI_tests.jpg/220px-Deep_Impact_HRI_tests.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Deep_Impact_HRI_tests.jpg/330px-Deep_Impact_HRI_tests.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Deep_Impact_HRI_tests.jpg/440px-Deep_Impact_HRI_tests.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Deep_Impact_HRI_tests.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cameras of the Flyby spacecraft, HRI at right, MRI at left</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Delta_II_7925_(2925)_rocket_with_Deep_Impact.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3008" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Delta_II_7925_%282925%29_rocket_with_Deep_Impact.jpg/170px-Delta_II_7925_%282925%29_rocket_with_Deep_Impact.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Delta_II_7925_%282925%29_rocket_with_Deep_Impact.jpg/255px-Delta_II_7925_%282925%29_rocket_with_Deep_Impact.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Delta_II_7925_%282925%29_rocket_with_Deep_Impact.jpg/340px-Delta_II_7925_%282925%29_rocket_with_Deep_Impact.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Delta_II_7925_(2925)_rocket_with_Deep_Impact.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Deep Impact</i> prior to launch on a <a href="/wiki/Delta_II" title="Delta II">Delta II</a> rocket</div></div></div>\n<p>Following its launch from <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral_Air_Force_Station" title="Cape Canaveral Air Force Station">Cape Canaveral Air Force Station</a> pad <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral_Air_Force_Station_Space_Launch_Complex_17" title="Cape Canaveral Air Force Station Space Launch Complex 17">SLC-17B</a> at 18:47 UTC on January 12, 2005,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SFN_Over_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-SFN_Over-4">[4]</a></sup> the <i>Deep Impact</i> spacecraft traveled 429&#160;million&#160;km (267&#160;million&#160;mi) in 174 days to reach comet Tempel 1 at a cruising speed of 28.6&#160;km/s (103,000&#160;km/h; 64,000&#160;mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Milems_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-Milems-6">[6]</a></sup> Once the spacecraft reached the vicinity of the comet on July 3, 2005, it separated into the Impactor and Flyby sections. The Impactor used its thrusters to move into the path of the comet, impacting 24 hours later at a relative speed of 10.3&#160;km/s (37,000&#160;km/h; 23,000&#160;mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Milems_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-Milems-6">[6]</a></sup> The Impactor delivered <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7010196000000000000&#9824;</span>1.96<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>10</sup>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">joule</a></span>s of <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a>&#8212;the equivalent of 4.7 <a href="/wiki/Ton" title="Ton">tons</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trinitrotoluene" title="Trinitrotoluene">TNT</a>. Scientists believed that the energy of the high-velocity collision would be sufficient to excavate a crater up to 100&#160;m (330&#160;ft) wide, larger than the bowl of the Roman <a href="/wiki/Colosseum" title="Colosseum">Colosseum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Milems_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-Milems-6">[6]</a></sup> The size of the crater was still not known one year after the impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes30_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes30-16">[16]</a></sup>  The 2007 <a href="/wiki/Stardust_(spacecraft)" title="Stardust (spacecraft)">Stardust</a> spacecraft\'s <a href="/wiki/Tempel_1#NExT_mission" title="Tempel 1">NExT mission</a> determined the crater\'s diameter to be 150 meters (490&#160;ft).\n</p><p>Just minutes after the impact, the Flyby probe passed by the nucleus at a close distance of 500&#160;km (310&#160;mi), taking pictures of the crater position, the <a href="/wiki/Ejecta" title="Ejecta">ejecta plume</a>, and the entire cometary nucleus. The entire event was also photographed by Earth-based telescopes and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_observatory" title="Space observatory">orbital observatories</a>, including <i><a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Chandra_X-ray_Observatory" title="Chandra X-ray Observatory">Chandra</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Spitzer_Space_Telescope" title="Spitzer Space Telescope">Spitzer</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/XMM-Newton" title="XMM-Newton">XMM-Newton</a></i>. The impact was also observed by cameras and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spectroscope" title="Spectroscope">spectroscopes</a> on board Europe\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rosetta_spacecraft" title="Rosetta spacecraft"><i>Rosetta</i> spacecraft</a>, which was about 80&#160;million&#160;km (50&#160;million&#160;mi) from the comet at the time of impact. <i>Rosetta</i> determined the composition of the gas and <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">dust</a> cloud that was kicked up by the impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESA_portal_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESA_portal-17">[17]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_events">Mission events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Mission events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Animation_of_Deep_Impact_trajectory.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="560" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Animation_of_Deep_Impact_trajectory.gif/250px-Animation_of_Deep_Impact_trajectory.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Animation_of_Deep_Impact_trajectory.gif/375px-Animation_of_Deep_Impact_trajectory.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Animation_of_Deep_Impact_trajectory.gif/500px-Animation_of_Deep_Impact_trajectory.gif 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Animation_of_Deep_Impact_trajectory.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animation of <i>Deep Impact</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> trajectory from January 12, 2005, to August 8, 2013 <br/> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:magenta; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<i>Deep Impact</i></span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:lime; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Tempel_1" title="Tempel 1">Tempel 1</a></span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:royalblue; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a></span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:cyan; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/103P/Hartley" title="103P/Hartley">103P/Hartley</a></span></div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Before_launch">Before launch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Before launch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>A comet-impact mission was first proposed to NASA in 1996, but at the time, NASA engineers were skeptical that the target could be hit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA3_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA3-18">[18]</a></sup> In 1999, a revised and technologically upgraded mission proposal, dubbed <i>Deep Impact</i>, was accepted and funded as part of NASA\'s <a href="/wiki/Discovery_Program" title="Discovery Program">Discovery Program</a> of low-cost spacecraft. The two spacecraft (Impactor and Flyby) and the three main instruments were built and integrated by <a href="/wiki/Ball_Aerospace_%26_Technologies" title="Ball Aerospace &amp; Technologies">Ball Aerospace &amp; Technologies</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ball_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ball-19">[19]</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Boulder,_Colorado" title="Boulder, Colorado">Boulder, Colorado</a>. Developing the software for the spacecraft took 18 months and the application code consisted of 20,000 lines and 19 different application threads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Milems_6-5"><a href="#cite_note-Milems-6">[6]</a></sup> The total cost of developing the spacecraft and completing its mission reached <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$330 million</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNNDI_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-CNNDI-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch_and_commissioning_phase">Launch and commissioning phase</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Launch and commissioning phase">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The probe was originally scheduled for launch on December 30, 2004, but NASA officials delayed its launch, in order to allow more time for testing the software.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SpaceFlightNow_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-SpaceFlightNow-21">[21]</a></sup> It was successfully launched from <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral" title="Cape Canaveral">Cape Canaveral</a> on January 12, 2005 at 1:47&#160;pm EST (1847 UTC) by a <a href="/wiki/Delta_II" title="Delta II">Delta II</a> rocket.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boeing_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boeing-22">[22]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>Deep Impact</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> state of health was uncertain during the first day after launch. Shortly after entering orbit around the Sun and deploying its solar panels, the probe switched itself to <a href="/wiki/Safe_mode_(spacecraft)" title="Safe mode (spacecraft)">safe mode</a>. The cause of the problem was simply an incorrect temperature limit in the fault protection logic for the spacecraft\'s <a href="/wiki/Reaction_control_system" title="Reaction control system">RCS thruster</a> catalyst beds. The spacecraft\'s thrusters were used to detumble the spacecraft following third stage separation. On January 13, 2005, NASA announced that the probe was out of safe mode and healthy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA4_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA4-23">[23]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On February 11, 2005, <i>Deep Impact</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> rockets were fired as planned to correct the spacecraft\'s course. This correction was so precise that the next planned correction maneuver on March 31, 2005, was unnecessary and canceled. The "commissioning phase" verified that all instruments were activated and checked out. During these tests it was found that the HRI images were not in focus after it underwent a <a href="/wiki/Bake-out" title="Bake-out">bake-out</a> period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spaceflight_Now_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spaceflight_Now-24">[24]</a></sup> After mission members investigated the problem, on June 9, 2005, it was announced that by using image processing software and the mathematical technique of <a href="/wiki/Deconvolution#Optics_and_other_imaging" title="Deconvolution">deconvolution</a>, the HRI images could be corrected to restore much of the resolution anticipated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Space-25">[25]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cruise_phase">Cruise phase</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Cruise phase">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA07879.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="750" data-file-width="750" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/PIA07879.jpg/220px-PIA07879.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/PIA07879.jpg/330px-PIA07879.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/PIA07879.jpg/440px-PIA07879.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA07879.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comet <a href="/wiki/Tempel_1" title="Tempel 1">Tempel 1</a> imaged on April 25 by the <i>Deep Impact</i> spacecraft</div></div></div>\n<table class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="415" data-file-width="759" decoding="async" height="22" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Wikinews-logo.svg/40px-Wikinews-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Wikinews-logo.svg/60px-Wikinews-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Wikinews-logo.svg/80px-Wikinews-logo.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist">Wikinews has related news:\n<i><b><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikinews.org/wiki/Deep_Impact_cruising_for_comet_crash" title="wikinews:Deep Impact cruising for comet crash"> Deep Impact cruising for comet crash</a></b></i></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The "cruise phase" began on March 25, 2005, immediately after the commissioning phase was completed. This phase continued until about 60 days before the encounter with comet Tempel 1. On April 25, 2005, the probe acquired the first image of its target at a distance of 64&#160;million&#160;km (40&#160;million&#160;mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JPL1_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-JPL1-26">[26]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On May 4, 2005, the spacecraft executed its second trajectory correction maneuver. Burning its rocket engine for 95 seconds, the spacecraft speed was changed by 18.2&#160;km/h (11.3&#160;mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spaceref_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spaceref-27">[27]</a></sup> Rick Grammier, the project manager for the mission at NASA\'s Jet Propulsion Laboratory, reacted to the maneuver stating that "spacecraft performance has been excellent, and this burn was no different... it was a textbook maneuver that placed us right on the money."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spaceref_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Spaceref-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Approach_phase">Approach phase</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Approach phase">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The approach phase extended from 60 days before encounter (May 5, 2005) until five days before encounter. Sixty days out was the earliest time that the <i>Deep Impact</i> spacecraft was expected to detect the comet with its MRI camera. In fact, the comet was spotted ahead of schedule, 69 days before impact (see <a href="#Cruise_phase">Cruise phase</a> above). This milestone marks the beginning of an intensive period of observations to refine knowledge of the comet\'s orbit and study the comet\'s rotation, activity, and dust environment.\n</p><p>On June 14 and 22, 2005, <i>Deep Impact</i> observed two outbursts of activity from the comet, the latter being six times larger than the former.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JPL2_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-JPL2-28">[28]</a></sup> The spacecraft studied the images of various distant stars to determine its current trajectory and position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Milems_6-6"><a href="#cite_note-Milems-6">[6]</a></sup> Don Yeomans, a mission co-investigator for JPL pointed out that "it takes 7&#189; minutes for the signal to get back to Earth, so you cannot joystick this thing. You have to rely on the fact that the Impactor is a smart spacecraft as is the Flyby spacecraft. So you have to build in the intelligence ahead of time and let it do its thing."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SpaceBull_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-SpaceBull-29">[29]</a></sup> On June 23, 2005, the first of the two final trajectory correct maneuvers (targeting maneuver) was successfully executed. A 6&#160;m/s (20&#160;ft/s) velocity change was needed to adjust the flight path towards the comet and target the Impactor at a window in space about 100 kilometers (62&#160;mi) wide.\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DI_MRI_T1_doy150.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="120" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/DI_MRI_T1_doy150.jpg/180px-DI_MRI_T1_doy150.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/DI_MRI_T1_doy150.jpg/240px-DI_MRI_T1_doy150.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>May 30, 2005, 35 days from impact\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA03297.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="492" data-file-width="492" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/PIA03297.jpg/120px-PIA03297.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/PIA03297.jpg/180px-PIA03297.jpg 1.5x, impact\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA02101.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/PIA02101.jpg/120px-PIA02101.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/PIA02101.jpg/180px-PIA02101.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/PIA02101.jpg/240px-PIA02101.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>June 21, 13 days from impact\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DI_MRI_log_T1_doy178.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="516" data-file-width="516" decoding="async" height="120" impact, near end of approach phase\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_phase">Impact phase</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Impact phase">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Deep_Impact_encounter_sequence.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1922" data-file-width="3385" decoding="async" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Deep_Impact_encounter_sequence.png/220px-Deep_Impact_encounter_sequence.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Deep_Impact_encounter_sequence.png/330px-Deep_Impact_encounter_sequence.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Deep_Impact_encounter_sequence.png/440px-Deep_Impact_encounter_sequence.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Deep_Impact_encounter_sequence.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Deep Impact</i> comet encounter sequence</div></div></div>\n<p>Impact phase began nominally on June 29, 2005, five days before impact. The Impactor successfully separated from the Flyby spacecraft on July 3 at 6:00 UTC (6:07 UTC <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth-received_time" title="Earth-received time">ERT</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deep_Impact_Home_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Deep_Impact_Home-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bloomberg_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bloomberg-31">[31]</a></sup> The first images from the instrumented Impactor were seen two hours after separation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DICE_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-DICE-32">[32]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Flyby spacecraft performed one of two divert maneuvers to avoid damage. A 14-minute burn was executed which slowed down the spacecraft. It was also reported that the communication link between the Flyby and the Impactor was functioning as expected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA4_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA4-23">[23]</a></sup> The Impactor executed three correction maneuvers in the final two hours before impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA5_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA5-33">[33]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Impactor was maneuvered to plant itself in front of the comet, so that Tempel 1 would collide with it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASAQ&amp;A_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-NASAQ&amp;A-7">[7]</a></sup> Impact occurred at 05:45 UTC (05:52 UTC <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth-received_time" title="Earth-received time">ERT</a>, +/- up to three minutes, one-way light time = 7m 26s) on the morning of July 4, 2005, within one second of the expected time for impact.\n</p><p>The impactor returned images as late as three seconds before impact. Most of the data captured was stored on board the Flyby spacecraft, which radioed approximately 4,500 images from the HRI, MRI, and ITS cameras to Earth over the next few days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC1_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC1-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA6_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA6-35">[35]</a></sup> The energy from the collision was similar in size to exploding five tons of <a href="/wiki/Dynamite" title="Dynamite">dynamite</a> and the comet shone six times brighter than normal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wired_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wired-36">[36]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A mission timeline is located at <a class="external text" href="http://www.nasa.gov/mission_pages/deepimpact/timeline/index.html" rel="nofollow">Impact Phase Timeline</a> (NASA).\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA02112.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/PIA02112.jpg/120px-PIA02112.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/PIA02112.jpg/180px-PIA02112.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/PIA02112.jpg/240px-PIA02112.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Comet <a href="/wiki/Tempel_1" title="Tempel 1">Tempel 1</a>, imaged from 4.2&#160;million km at the start of Impact phase\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DI_MRI_Impactor_-24hr.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1072" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" Impactor\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" image, taken shortly before impact\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:17px as shown on <a href="/wiki/NASA_TV" title="NASA TV">NASA TV</a>\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: impact\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" class="image" href="/wiki/File:ITS_Impact.gif"><img alt="" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="120" during encounter (video)\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Results">Results</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Results">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Deep_Impact_celebration.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="384" data-file-width="516" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Deep_Impact_celebration.jpg/220px-Deep_Impact_celebration.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Deep_Impact_celebration.jpg/330px-Deep_Impact_celebration.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Deep_Impact_celebration.jpg/440px-Deep_Impact_celebration.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Deep_Impact_celebration.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mission team members celebrate after the impact with the comet</div></div></div>\n<table class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="415" data-file-width="759" decoding="async" height="22" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Wikinews-logo.svg/40px-Wikinews-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Wikinews-logo.svg/60px-Wikinews-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Wikinews-logo.svg/80px-Wikinews-logo.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist">Wikinews has related news:\n<i><b><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikinews.org/wiki/NASA%27s_Deep_Impact_probe_strikes_comet_successfully" title="wikinews:NASA\'s Deep Impact probe strikes comet successfully"> NASA\'s Deep Impact probe strikes comet successfully</a></b></i></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Mission control did not become aware of the Impactor\'s success until five minutes later at 05:57 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UTC" title="UTC">UTC</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNNDI_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-CNNDI-20">[20]</a></sup> Don Yeomans confirmed the results for the press, "We hit it just exactly where we wanted to"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NewRussia_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-NewRussia-37">[37]</a></sup> and JPL Director Charles Elachi stated "The success exceeded our expectations."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CriEnglish_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-CriEnglish-38">[38]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the post-impact briefing on July 4, 2005, at 08:00 UTC, the first processed images revealed existing <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">craters</a> on the comet. NASA scientists stated they could not see the new crater that had formed from the Impactor, but it was later discovered to be about 100&#160;meters wide and up to 30 meters (98&#160;ft) deep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NewScientist_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-NewScientist-39">[39]</a></sup> Lucy McFadden, one of the co-investigators of the impact, stated "We didn\'t expect the success of one part of the mission [bright dust cloud] to affect a second part [seeing the resultant crater]. But that is part of the fun of science, to meet with the unexpected."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MissResults_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-MissResults-40">[40]</a></sup> Analysis of data from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swift_Gamma-Ray_Burst_Mission" title="Swift Gamma-Ray Burst Mission"><i>Swift</i> X-ray telescope</a> showed that the comet continued outgassing from the impact for 13 days, with a peak five days after impact. A total of 5&#160;million&#160;kg (11&#160;million&#160;lb) of water<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_News_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News-41">[41]</a></sup> and between 10 and 25&#160;million&#160;kg (22 and 55&#160;million&#160;lb) of dust were lost from the impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NewScientist_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-NewScientist-39">[39]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Initial results were surprising as the material excavated by the impact contained more dust and less ice than had been expected. The only models of cometary structure astronomers could positively rule out were the very porous ones which had comets as loose aggregates of material. In addition, the material was finer than expected; scientists compared it to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Talcum_powder" title="Talcum powder">talcum powder</a> rather than <a href="/wiki/Sand" title="Sand">sand</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VPNews_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-VPNews-42">[42]</a></sup> Other materials found while studying the impact included <a href="/wiki/Clay" title="Clay">clays</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a>, and crystalline <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicates</a> which were found by studying the spectroscopy of the impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes30_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes30-16">[16]</a></sup> Clays and carbonates usually require liquid water to form and sodium is rare in space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AstroBiology.com_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-AstroBiology.com-43">[43]</a></sup> Observations also revealed that the comet was about 75% empty space, and one astronomer compared the outer layers of the comet to the same makeup of a snow bank.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes30_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes30-16">[16]</a></sup> Astronomers have expressed interest in more missions to different comets to determine if they share similar compositions or if there are different materials found deeper within comets that were produced at the time of the Solar System\'s formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C&amp;EN_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-C&amp;EN-44">[44]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DeepImpactTempelCrater.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="797" data-file-width="1233" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/DeepImpactTempelCrater.jpg/220px-DeepImpactTempelCrater.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/DeepImpactTempelCrater.jpg/330px-DeepImpactTempelCrater.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/DeepImpactTempelCrater.jpg/440px-DeepImpactTempelCrater.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DeepImpactTempelCrater.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\'Before and after\' comparison images from <i>Deep Impact</i> and <i>Stardust</i>, showing the crater formed by <i>Deep Impact</i> on the right hand image.</div></div></div>\n<p>Astronomers hypothesized, based on its interior chemistry, that the comet formed in the <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a> <a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">Oort cloud</a> region of the Solar System. A comet which forms farther from the Sun is expected to have greater amounts of ices with low freezing temperatures, such as <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a>, which was present in Tempel 1. Astronomers believe that other comets with compositions similar to Tempel 1 are likely to have formed in the same region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crater">Crater</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Crater">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Because the quality of the images of the crater formed during the <i>Deep Impact</i> collision was not satisfactory, on July 3, 2007, NASA approved the New Exploration of Tempel 1 (or NExT) mission. The mission utilized the already existing <a href="/wiki/Stardust_(spacecraft)" title="Stardust (spacecraft)"><i>Stardust</i> spacecraft</a>, which had studied <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comet_Wild_2" title="Comet Wild 2">Comet Wild 2</a> in 2004. <i>Stardust</i> was placed into a new orbit so that it passed by Tempel 1 at a distance of approximately 200&#160;km (120&#160;mi) on February 15, 2011, at 04:42 UTC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> This was the first time that a comet was visited by two probes on separate occasions (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1P/Halley" title="1P/Halley">1P/Halley</a> had been visited by several probes within a few weeks in 1986), and it provided an opportunity to better observe the crater that was created by <i>Deep Impact</i> as well as observing the changes caused by the comet\'s latest close approach to the Sun.\n</p><p>On February 15, NASA scientists identified the crater formed by <i>Deep Impact</i> in images from <i>Stardust</i>. The crater is estimated to be 150 meters (490&#160;ft) in diameter, and has a bright mound in the center likely created when material from the impact fell back into the crater.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_interest">Public interest</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Public interest">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media_coverage">Media coverage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Media coverage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Deep_Impact_HRI.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Deep_Impact_HRI.jpeg/220px-Deep_Impact_HRI.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Deep_Impact_HRI.jpeg/330px-Deep_Impact_HRI.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Deep_Impact_HRI.jpeg/440px-Deep_Impact_HRI.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Deep_Impact_HRI.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The image of the impact which was widely circulated in the media</div></div></div>\n<p>The impact was a substantial news event reported and discussed online, in print, and on television. There was a genuine suspense because experts held widely differing opinions over the result of the impact. Various experts debated whether the Impactor would go straight through the comet and out the other side, would create an impact crater, would open up a hole in the interior of the comet, and other theories. However, twenty-four hours before impact, the flight team at JPL began privately expressing a high level of confidence that, barring any unforeseen technical glitches, the spacecraft would intercept Tempel 1. One senior personnel member stated "All we can do now is sit back and wait. Everything we can technically do to ensure impact has been done." In the final minutes as the Impactor hit the comet, more than 10,000 people watched the collision on a giant movie screen at Hawaii\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waik%C4%ABk%C4%AB_Beach" title="Waik&#299;k&#299; Beach">Waik&#299;k&#299; Beach</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wired_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wired-36">[36]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Experts came up with a range of soundbites to summarize the mission to the public. Iwan Williams of <a href="/wiki/Queen_Mary_University_of_London" title="Queen Mary University of London">Queen Mary University of London</a>, said "It was like a mosquito hitting a <a href="/wiki/Boeing_747" title="Boeing 747">747</a>. What we\'ve found is that the mosquito didn\'t splat on the surface; it\'s actually gone through the windscreen."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCNews3_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBCNews3-48">[48]</a></sup>\n</p><p>One day after the impact Marina Bay, a Russian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astrologer" title="Astrologer">astrologer</a>, sued NASA for <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$300 million</span> for the impact which "ruin[ed] the natural balance of forces in the universe."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSNBCRussia_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-MSNBCRussia-49">[49]</a></sup> Her lawyer asked the public to volunteer to help in the claim by declaring "The impact changed the magnetic properties of the comet, and this could have affected mobile telephony here on Earth. If your phone went down this morning, ask yourself Why? and then get in touch with us."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCNews17_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBCNews17-50">[50]</a></sup> On August 9, 2005 the Presnensky Court of Moscow ruled against Bay, although she did attempt to appeal the result. One Russian physicist said that the impact had no effect on Earth and "the change to the orbit of the comet after the collision was only about 10&#160;cm (3.9&#160;in)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MosNews_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-MosNews-51">[51]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Send_Your_Name_To_A_Comet_campaign">Send Your Name To A Comet campaign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Send Your Name To A Comet campaign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Send_Your_Name_to_a_Comet_CD_on_Deep_Impact.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="980" data-file-width="1504" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Send_Your_Name_to_a_Comet_CD_on_Deep_Impact.jpg/220px-Send_Your_Name_to_a_Comet_CD_on_Deep_Impact.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Send_Your_Name_to_a_Comet_CD_on_Deep_Impact.jpg/330px-Send_Your_Name_to_a_Comet_CD_on_Deep_Impact.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Send_Your_Name_to_a_Comet_CD_on_Deep_Impact.jpg/440px-Send_Your_Name_to_a_Comet_CD_on_Deep_Impact.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Send_Your_Name_to_a_Comet_CD_on_Deep_Impact.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The CD containing the 625,000 names is added to the Impactor</div></div></div>\n<p>The mission was notable for one of its promotional campaigns, "Send Your Name To A Comet!". Visitors to the <a href="/wiki/Jet_Propulsion_Laboratory" title="Jet Propulsion Laboratory">Jet Propulsion Laboratory</a>\'s website were invited to submit their name between May 2003 and January 2004, and the names gathered&#8212;some 625,000 in all&#8212;were then burnt onto a mini-CD, which was attached to the Impactor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA7_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA7-52">[52]</a></sup> Dr. Don Yeomans, a member of the spacecraft\'s scientific team, stated "this is an opportunity to become part of an extraordinary space mission&#160;... when the craft is launched in December 2004, yours and the names of your loved-ones can hitch along for the ride and be part of what may be the best space fireworks show in history."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA8_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA8-53">[53]</a></sup> The idea was credited with driving interest in the mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space.com2_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Space.com2-54">[54]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reaction_from_China">Reaction from China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Reaction from China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Chinese researchers used the <i>Deep Impact</i> mission as an opportunity to highlight the efficiency of American science because public support ensured the possibility of funding long-term research. By contrast, "in China, the public usually has no idea what our scientists are doing, and limited funding for the promotion of science weakens people\'s enthusiasm for research."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-xinhuanet_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-xinhuanet-55">[55]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Two days after the US mission succeeded in having a probe collide with a comet, China revealed a plan for what it called a "more clever" version of the mission: landing a probe on a small comet or <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a> to push it off course.  China said it would begin the mission after sending a probe to the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EconTimes_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-EconTimes-56">[56]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contributions_from_amateur_astronomers">Contributions from amateur astronomers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Contributions from amateur astronomers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Deep_Impact_Discovery_Mission_to_Comet_Tempel_1.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="754" data-file-width="776" decoding="async" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Deep_Impact_Discovery_Mission_to_Comet_Tempel_1.PNG/220px-Deep_Impact_Discovery_Mission_to_Comet_Tempel_1.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Deep_Impact_Discovery_Mission_to_Comet_Tempel_1.PNG/330px-Deep_Impact_Discovery_Mission_to_Comet_Tempel_1.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Deep_Impact_Discovery_Mission_to_Comet_Tempel_1.PNG/440px-Deep_Impact_Discovery_Mission_to_Comet_Tempel_1.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Deep_Impact_Discovery_Mission_to_Comet_Tempel_1.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Deep Impact</i> participation certificate of Maciej Szczepa&#324;czyk</div></div></div>\n<p>Since observing time on large, professional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Telescopes" title="Telescopes">telescopes</a> such as <a href="/wiki/W._M._Keck_Observatory" title="W. M. Keck Observatory">Keck</a> or <i>Hubble</i> is always scarce, the <i>Deep&#160;Impact</i> scientists called upon "advanced amateur, student, and professional <a href="/wiki/Astronomer" title="Astronomer">astronomers</a>" to use small telescopes to make long-term observations of the target comet before and after impact. The purpose of these observations was to look for "volatile outgassing, dust coma development and dust production rates, dust tail development, and jet activity and outbursts."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AOP_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-AOP-57">[57]</a></sup> By mid-2007, amateur astronomers had submitted over a thousand <a href="/wiki/Charge-coupled_device" title="Charge-coupled device">CCD</a> images of the comet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-STSP_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-STSP-58">[58]</a></sup>\n</p><p>One notable amateur observation was by students from schools in Hawaii, working with US and UK scientists, who during the press conference took live images using the <a href="/wiki/Faulkes_Telescope_North" title="Faulkes Telescope North">Faulkes Automatic Telescope</a> in Hawaii (the students operated the telescope over the Internet) and were one of the first groups to get images of the impact. One amateur astronomer reported seeing a structureless bright cloud around the comet, and an estimated 2&#160;<a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">magnitude</a> increase in brightness after the impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Google_Groups_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Google_Groups-59">[59]</a></sup> Another amateur published a map of the crash area from NASA images.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jost_Jahn_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jost_Jahn-60">[60]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Musical_tribute">Musical tribute</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Musical tribute">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <i>Deep Impact</i> mission coincided with celebrations in the Los Angeles area marking the 50th&#160;anniversary of "<a href="/wiki/Rock_Around_the_Clock" title="Rock Around the Clock">Rock Around the Clock</a>" by <a href="/wiki/Bill_Haley_%26_His_Comets" title="Bill Haley &amp; His Comets">Bill Haley &amp; His Comets</a> becoming the first <a href="/wiki/Rock_and_roll" title="Rock and roll">rock and roll</a> single to reach No.&#160;1 on the recording sales charts. Within 24&#160;hours of the mission\'s success, a 2-minute music video produced by <a href="/wiki/Martin_Lewis_(humorist)" title="Martin Lewis (humorist)">Martin Lewis</a> had been created using images of the impact itself combined with computer animation of the <i>Deep Impact</i> probe in flight, interspersed with footage of Bill Haley &amp; His Comets performing in 1955 and the surviving original members of The Comets performing in March 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Extra_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Extra-61">[61]</a></sup> The video was posted to NASA\'s website for a couple of weeks afterwards.\n</p><p>On July 5, 2005, the surviving original members of The Comets (ranging in age from 71&#8211;84) performed a free concert for hundreds of employees of the Jet Propulsion Laboratory to help them celebrate the mission\'s success. This event received worldwide press attention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USAToday_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-USAToday-62">[62]</a></sup> In February 2006, the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a> citation that officially named asteroid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/79896_Billhaley" title="79896 Billhaley">79896 Billhaley</a> included a reference to the JPL concert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klet_Observatory_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Klet_Observatory-63">[63]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Extended_mission">Extended mission</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Extended mission">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/EPOXI" title="EPOXI">EPOXI</a></div>\n<p><i>Deep Impact</i> embarked on an extended mission designated EPOXI (Extrasolar Planet Observation and <i>Deep Impact</i> Extended Investigation) to visit other comets, after being put to sleep in 2005 upon completion of the Tempel 1 mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AOLhartley_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-AOLhartley-64">[64]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Comet_Boethin_plan">Comet Boethin plan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Comet Boethin plan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Its first extended visit was to do a flyby of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/85P/Boethin" title="85P/Boethin">Comet Boethin</a>, but with some complications. On July 21, 2005, <i>Deep Impact</i> executed a trajectory correction maneuver that allows the spacecraft to use Earth\'s gravity to begin a new mission in a path towards another comet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SDaily3_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-SDaily3-65">[65]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The original plan was for a December 5, 2008, flyby of Comet Boethin, coming within 700 kilometers (430&#160;mi) of the comet. Michael A\'Hearn, the <i>Deep Impact</i> team leader, explained "We propose to direct the spacecraft for a flyby of Comet Boethin to investigate whether the results found at Comet Tempel 1 are unique or are also found on other comets."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Skymania_News_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Skymania_News-66">[66]</a></sup> The $40&#160;million mission would provide about half of the information as the collision of Tempel 1 but at a fraction of the cost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Skymania_News_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-Skymania_News-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40Fox_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-40Fox-67">[67]</a></sup> <i>Deep Impact</i> would use its spectrometer to study the comet\'s surface composition and its telescope for viewing the surface features.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SDaily3_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-SDaily3-65">[65]</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, as the December 2007 Earth <a href="/wiki/Gravity_assist" title="Gravity assist">gravity assist</a> approached, astronomers were unable to locate Comet Boethin, which may have broken up into pieces too faint to be observed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPOXIMissionStatus_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-EPOXIMissionStatus-68">[68]</a></sup> Consequently, its orbit could not be calculated with sufficient precision to permit a flyby.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flyby_of_Comet_Hartley_2">Flyby of Comet Hartley 2</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Flyby of Comet Hartley 2">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NASAHartley2Comet.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="240" data-file-width="427" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/NASAHartley2Comet.jpg/220px-NASAHartley2Comet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/NASAHartley2Comet.jpg/330px-NASAHartley2Comet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/42/NASAHartley2Comet.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NASAHartley2Comet.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comet Hartley 2 on November 4, 2010</div></div></div>\n<p>In November 2007 the JPL team targeted <i>Deep Impact</i> toward Comet <a href="/wiki/103P/Hartley" title="103P/Hartley">Hartley 2</a>. However, this would require an extra two years of travel for <i>Deep Impact</i> (including earth gravity assists in December 2007 and December 2008).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPOXIMissionStatus_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-EPOXIMissionStatus-68">[68]</a></sup> On May 28, 2010, a burn of 11.3 seconds was conducted, to enable the June 27 Earth fly-by to be optimized for the transit to Hartley 2 and fly-by on November 4. The velocity change was 0.1&#160;m/s (0.33&#160;ft/s).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On November 4, 2010, the <i>Deep Impact</i> extended mission (EPOXI) returned images from comet Hartley 2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AOLhartley_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-AOLhartley-64">[64]</a></sup> EPOXI came within 700 kilometers (430&#160;mi) of the comet, returning detailed photographs of the "peanut" shaped cometary nucleus and several bright jets. The probe\'s medium-resolution instrument captured the photographs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AOLhartley_64-2"><a href="#cite_note-AOLhartley-64">[64]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Comet_Garradd_.28C.2F2009_P1.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Comet_Garradd_(C/2009_P1)">Comet Garradd (C/2009 P1)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Comet Garradd (C/2009 P1)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><i>Deep Impact</i> observed <a href="/wiki/Gordon_J._Garradd" title="Gordon J. Garradd">Comet Garradd (C/2009 P1)</a> from February 20 to April 8, 2012, using its Medium Resolution Instrument, through a variety of filters. The comet was 1.75&#8211;2.11&#160;<a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a> (262&#8211;316&#160;million&#160;km) from the Sun and 1.87&#8211;1.30&#160;<a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a> (280&#8211;194&#160;million&#160;km) from the spacecraft. It was found that the outgassing from the comet varies with a period of 10.4 hours, which is presumed to be due to the rotation of its nucleus. The dry ice content of the comet was measured and found to be about ten percent of its water ice content by number of molecules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPOXI_Mission_Status_Reports_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-EPOXI_Mission_Status_Reports-71">[71]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Possible_mission_to_asteroid_.28163249.29_2002_GT"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Possible_mission_to_asteroid_(163249)_2002_GT">Possible mission to asteroid (163249) 2002 GT</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Possible mission to asteroid (163249) 2002 GT">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>At the end of 2011, <i>Deep Impact</i> was re-targeted towards asteroid <a href="/wiki/(163249)_2002_GT" title="(163249) 2002 GT">(163249) 2002 GT</a> which it would reach in January 2020. At the time of re-targeting, whether or not a related science mission would be carried out in 2020 was yet to be determined, based on NASA\'s budget and the health of the probe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2002GT_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-2002GT-72">[72]</a></sup> A 71-second engine burn on October 4, 2012, changed the probe\'s velocity by 2&#160;m/s (6.6&#160;ft/s) to keep the mission on track.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2002GT2012_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-2002GT2012-73">[73]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Comet_C.2F2012_S1_.28ISON.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Comet_C/2012_S1_(ISON)">Comet C/2012 S1 (ISON)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Comet C/2012 S1 (ISON)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In February 2013, <i>Deep Impact</i> observed <a href="/wiki/Comet_ISON" title="Comet ISON">Comet ISON</a>. The comet remained observable until March 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISON-UT_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-ISON-UT-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ARXIV-20140508_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-ARXIV-20140508-75">[75]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contact_lost_and_end_of_mission">Contact lost and end of mission</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Contact lost and end of mission">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>On September 3, 2013, a mission update was posted to the EPOXI mission status website, stating "Communication with the spacecraft was lost some time between August 11 and August 14 ... The last communication was on August 8. ... the team on August 30 determined the cause of the problem. The team is now trying to determine how best to try to recover communication."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPOXI_Mission_Status_Reports_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-EPOXI_Mission_Status_Reports-71">[71]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On September 10, 2013, a <i>Deep Impact</i> mission status report explained that mission controllers believe the computers on the spacecraft are continuously rebooting themselves and so are unable to issue any commands to the vehicle\'s thrusters. As a result of this problem, communication with the spacecraft was explained to be more difficult, as the orientation of the vehicle\'s antennas is unknown. Additionally, the solar panels on the vehicle may no longer be positioned correctly for generating power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On September 20, 2013, NASA abandoned further attempts to contact the craft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> According to chief scientist A\'Hearn,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> the reason for the software malfunction was a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Y2K" title="Y2K">Y2K</a>-like problem. August 11, 2013, 00:38:49, was 2<sup>32</sup> of one-tenth seconds from January 1, 2000, leading to speculation that a system on the craft tracked time in one-tenth second increments since January 1, 2000, and stored it in a <a href="/wiki/Integer_(computer_science)" title="Integer (computer science)">signed 32-bit integer</a>, which then <a href="/wiki/Integer_overflow" title="Integer overflow">overflowed</a> at this time, similar to the <a href="/wiki/Year_2038_problem" title="Year 2038 problem">Year 2038 problem</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-y2k_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-y2k-79">[79]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div
